This unique structural advantage allows endowment managers to embrace illiquidity premiums, invest in long-gestation private markets, and ignore short-term market noise in a way that retail or pension managers simply cannot, leveraging their infinite horizon to capture returns that are unavailable to investors with shorter time horizons or mandatory liquidity requirements. For example, an endowment can commit capital to a ten-year private equity vintage or a illiquid infrastructure project, knowing that the institution will not face a sudden, unexpected demand for that capital, whereas a defined benefit pension fund must maintain high liquidity to meet monthly benefit payments to retirees. This structural patience is the endowment's greatest weapon in the pursuit of superior risk-adjusted returns.

3. Types of Endowment Capital

Most organizations manage more than one kind of capital inside the endowment umbrella, and failing to distinguish between them with absolute precision is a recipe for legal, accounting, and reputational disaster. Donor-restricted endowment funds, often called true endowments, are established by gifts, grants, or bequests that carry explicit legal or donor-imposed conditions requiring the principal to be maintained in perpetuity or for a specified term, meaning the organization can typically only spend the investment income or a calculated portion of the total return, while the original gift amount must be preserved intact. For instance, a $5 million bequest from an alumnus to establish a named professorship in physics is a true endowment; the university must preserve the $5 million corpus in perpetuity and can only spend the annual distribution generated by the investment returns to support the professor's salary and research.

Board-designated endowment, often called quasi-endowment or funds functioning as endowment, consists of internal designations made by the board of directors or governing body, representing unrestricted funds that the board has chosen to set aside and invest with a long-term mindset, mimicking the behavior of a true endowment. However, because they lack a donor restriction, the board can usually redesignate or spend the principal at any time by a simple majority vote, subject to the organization's bylaws, making them a crucial source of financial flexibility during times of institutional stress. The IRS instructions and Schedule D reporting framework strictly recognize these distinctions because the source of the funds and the nature of the restriction determine exactly how the fund must be handled, invested, and publicly disclosed, with severe penalties for misclassification or misuse. The practical consequence of this classification system is that identical-looking dollars in the common investment pool may have vastly different degrees of legal and operational flexibility, creating a complex matrix of rights and obligations that must be meticulously tracked.

Additionally, organizations must manage term endowments, where the principal is restricted for a specific period rather than in perpetuity (e.g., a fund restricted for 20 years, after which it becomes unrestricted), and loan funds or annuity funds, which have specific payout structures and legal requirements. Charitable gift annuities and pooled income funds represent another layer of complexity; these are split-interest agreements where the institution receives a charitable deduction but is legally obligated to make fixed payments to the donor or their beneficiaries for the duration of their lives, requiring the institution to maintain a separate, highly liquid, and conservatively invested reserve to meet those actuarial liabilities. Sound management therefore begins with a flawless, auditable classification of every single dollar at the moment it enters the system, because a classification error is not only an accounting problem; it is a catastrophic governance failure that can distort spending decisions, violate trust, trigger regulatory penalties, and ultimately undermine the institution's ability to fulfill its mission.

4. Legal Framework and UPMIFA

The central legal framework governing endowment investments and expenditures in the United States is UPMIFA, the Uniform Prudent Management of Institutional Funds Act, which was drafted by the Uniform Law Commission and adopted by the vast majority of states, fundamentally modernizing the legal landscape for institutional investors. UPMIFA is critically important because it replaced older, rigid approaches, specifically the older UMIFA standards, that were fundamentally incompatible with contemporary modern portfolio theory and the realities of global financial markets. Prior to UPMIFA, under the older UMIFA standards, fiduciaries were often constrained by a "historic dollar value" floor, meaning they could not spend from an endowment if the market value fell below the original value of the gift, regardless of how prudent the spending might otherwise be. This "underwater" trap forced institutions into overly conservative investment strategies, heavily weighting portfolios toward fixed income to avoid nominal losses, which ultimately failed to keep pace with inflation and eroded the real value of the corpus over time.

UPMIFA abolished this rigid historic dollar value test in favor of a comprehensive "prudence" standard, giving fiduciaries the necessary room to exercise professional judgment while still requiring prudence, loyalty, and strict attention to donor intent. The Uniform Law Commission describes UPMIFA as governing funds donated to charitable institutions under modern investment and expenditure practice, and NACUBO’s UPMIFA resources organize endowment guidance around this exact framework, emphasizing that prudence is a process, not a specific outcome. Crucially, UPMIFA does not reduce stewardship to a single mechanical rule or a fixed percentage payout; instead, it directs decision-makers to consider seven specific factors when making spending and investment decisions: the duration and preservation of the fund, the purposes of the institution and the fund, general economic conditions, the possible effect of inflation or deflation, the expected total return from income and appreciation, the other resources of the institution, and the institution's investment policy. This holistic approach ensures that decisions are contextualized within the broader economic environment and the specific needs of the institution.

This is the right framework for endowments because long-term capital is simply not managed well by simplistic, one-size-fits-all formulas, and prudence requires deep context and a comprehensive understanding of the institutional environment. The law asks whether decisions are reasonable in light of the institution’s unique mission, risk tolerance, and specific circumstances, which is a vastly higher, more nuanced, and more useful standard than a narrow, backward-looking accounting test. By adopting UPMIFA, states enabled institutions to embrace total return investing strategies, allowing them to optimize for long-term real growth while respecting the legal boundaries of donor restrictions and fiduciary duty. However, this flexibility comes with a heightened burden of documentation; boards must meticulously record their deliberations, demonstrating that they explicitly considered the seven factors of prudence before setting spending rates or altering asset allocations, particularly when navigating periods of market distress or when managing underwater funds.

5. Donor Intent and Restriction Hierarchy

Donor intent is the absolute anchor of endowment stewardship and the legal compass by which all investment and spending decisions must be navigated, representing the sacred trust placed in the institution by those who have provided the capital. A donor may restrict a fund for a highly specific purpose, such as funding scholarships for students from a particular geographic region, supporting a specific chair in a specific academic department, maintaining a particular historic building, or advancing a specific line of medical research, and those terms matter profoundly because they shape exactly what the organization may and may not do with the gift, and they survive the death of the donor. FASB’s not-for-profit guidance, particularly the standards surrounding net asset classification under ASC 958, requires extensive disclosure around endowment composition, spending policies, and investment policies precisely because financial statement users need to understand how these legal restrictions affect the institution's available resources and future cash flows. Reporting is not a side task here; it is a fundamental part of honoring the legal and moral promise embedded in the gift, ensuring that the public and the donors can verify that their intentions are being fulfilled.

In practice, management must rigorously distinguish between donor restriction and board preference, as a board can often change its own designation of a quasi-endowment to meet emerging needs, but it cannot casually rewrite, ignore, or modify a donor’s legally binding condition without facing severe legal and reputational consequences. That hierarchy protects public trust, ensures compliance with state trust laws, and protects the institution from the dangerous, unethical habit of treating all endowment balances as fungible pools of cash that can be redirected at will. The more carefully an organization documents restriction, tracks the original gift agreement, and enforces the restriction hierarchy, the less likely it is to confuse legal limitation with internal convenience, thereby safeguarding its reputation and its relationship with current and prospective donors. Donor intent is not merely sentimental language in a fundraising brochure; it is a binding governing fact that dictates the permissible use of the capital in perpetuity.

When a donor's specific purpose becomes impossible, illegal, or impracticable to fulfill due to changes in circumstances—such as a fund established to support a specific department that is later dissolved—the organization must navigate complex legal doctrines such as cy pres or seek variance power from the donor or the state attorney general. Cy pres allows a court to redirect the funds to a purpose that "comes as near as possible" to the original donor's intent, but this process is lengthy, expensive, and highly public, often resulting in negative press if the institution is perceived as disregarding the donor's wishes. Alternatively, some gift agreements include a "variance power" clause, granting the board the explicit right to modify the restriction if the original purpose becomes unfeasible, provided the board consults with the donor or their heirs. Good endowment administration begins by making the fact of donor intent visible, respected, and enforceable in every policy, accounting entry, and public communication, ensuring that the institution remains a faithful steward of the resources entrusted to it.

6. Board Governance and Fiduciary Duty

Endowment management is ultimately a board responsibility, even when the day-to-day execution of the work is heavily delegated to professional staff, external consultants, investment committees, auditors, and custodian banks, as the board holds the ultimate fiduciary duty to the institution and its beneficiaries. AGB’s guidance emphasizes that boards and investment committees have direct, non-delegable stewardship duties in higher education endowment management, and the Council on Foundations similarly frames endowment oversight as a core governance function, not merely a technical investment function, meaning the board cannot absolve itself of responsibility by simply hiring an external manager. The board is responsible for setting the policy environment, defining the risk appetite, and ensuring the institutional infrastructure exists to make good decisions possible, acting as the ultimate guardian of the institution's long-term financial health. It is not expected to execute every trade or pick individual mutual funds, but it is absolutely expected to oversee the system that makes prudent management possible, ensuring that the investment strategy aligns with the institution's mission, liquidity needs, and risk tolerance.

Fiduciary duty in this context encompasses three distinct legal obligations: the duty of care (acting with the care, skill, and diligence of a prudent investor, which includes seeking expert advice and conducting thorough due diligence), the duty of loyalty (acting solely in the interest of the institution, avoiding conflicts of interest, and ensuring that all fees and compensation are reasonable), and the duty of obedience (ensuring the fund is used for its intended charitable purposes and in compliance with all applicable laws and donor restrictions). The board must understand the fund’s purpose, risk tolerance, liquidity needs, spending policy, and reporting structure, and must be willing to ask difficult questions and challenge assumptions when necessary. Good governance also means resisting the temptation to improvise or panic during periods of market stress, as the moment of a financial crisis is the absolute worst time to invent a new operating philosophy or abandon a carefully crafted asset allocation. Proper oversight is built before markets turn, which is why board education, detailed committee charters, rigorous meeting materials, and periodic policy reviews are not administrative extras; they are the essential infrastructure of prudence that protects the institution from its own potential impulsivity.

Furthermore, UPMIFA explicitly allows for the delegation of investment and management functions, but it requires the board to exercise prudence in selecting the agent, establishing the scope of the delegation, and periodically reviewing the agent's actions to ensure compliance with the terms of the delegation and the overarching goals of the institution. This means the board must regularly evaluate the performance of external investment consultants, custodians, and asset managers, ensuring that their fees are competitive, their strategies remain aligned with the IPS, and they are not taking on hidden risks that could jeopardize the fund. The board must also ensure that there is a clear segregation of duties within the institution's own staff, preventing any single individual from having unchecked control over trade execution, custody, and reconciliation, thereby mitigating the risk of fraud or catastrophic error.

7. Investment Policy Statement

The investment policy statement, universally known as the IPS, is the foundational, governing operating document for all endowment investing activities, serving as the constitutional framework that guides every investment decision and strategic allocation. CFA Institute’s institutional IPS guidance stresses that an IPS should clearly define objectives, constraints, governance structures, and review processes, and that rigorous structure fits endowment portfolios especially well because it provides a disciplined approach to managing complex, long-term capital. A strong, comprehensive IPS identifies the mission objective, the specific return objective (often expressed in real, inflation-adjusted terms to ensure purchasing power preservation), the explicit risk tolerance, the benchmark framework, the liquidity needs, the rebalancing rules, the criteria for selecting and terminating external managers, and the exact lines of authority and delegation. It is not a decorative document to be signed and filed away; it is the written, binding expression of how the institution intends to behave when the market stops cooperating and volatility spikes, providing a pre-commitment mechanism that prevents emotional, reactive decision-making.

A well-designed IPS protects the organization from the natural human tendency to panic during market downturns or become overly greedy during bull markets, keeping the board from confusing short-term market volatility with long-term strategic failure, and keeping staff from drifting into habits or asset classes that were never approved. The best IPS documents are highly specific to the institution's mission and capital structure, recognizing that an endowment supporting annual scholarships, which requires high liquidity and stable returns, may need different liquidity and risk limits than an endowment with a much longer spending horizon, such as a museum preservation fund or a climate change research initiative. The IPS should make those differences explicit so that every party, from the board members to the external managers, can see the rules before the pressure arrives, ensuring alignment and accountability. Additionally, the IPS must address the treatment of Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG) factors if they are material to the long-term risk and return profile of the portfolio, ensuring that mission alignment and ethical considerations are integrated into the investment process without inadvertently breaching fiduciary duties or compromising financial performance.

The IPS should be a living document, reviewed and updated regularly to reflect changes in the institutional mission, market conditions, regulatory environment, and the evolving understanding of risk and return, ensuring that it remains relevant and effective as a tool for governance and stewardship. However, changes to the IPS should not be made lightly or in response to short-term market noise; they should be the result of a deliberate, strategic review process, typically conducted annually or biennially by the investment committee, with input from external consultants and legal counsel. When the IPS is clearly articulated and rigorously enforced, it serves as the ultimate defense against claims of imprudence, as it demonstrates that the board acted with foresight, discipline, and a clear mandate from the institution's governing documents.

8. Spending Policy and Payout Discipline

Spending policy is the precise mechanism that turns abstract investment returns into tangible mission support, and it is also the mechanism that determines whether an endowment is sustainably managed or quietly consumed over time, making it one of the most critical decisions in endowment governance. UPMIFA’s prudence framework encourages an approach that balances current support against the preservation of long-term purchasing power, recognizing that the needs of current beneficiaries must be met without compromising the ability of future beneficiaries to benefit from the fund. That means the organization should not spend only from current income (which often fails to keep pace with inflation and forces a conservative, equity-light portfolio that may not generate sufficient long-term growth), and it should not spend so aggressively from total return that future capacity is damaged and the real value of the corpus is eroded. The correct target is sustainability, avoiding either extreme of hoarding capital to the detriment of current programs or depleting the fund through excessive payouts.

The form of the payout formula can vary significantly depending on the institution's risk tolerance, operational needs, and donor restrictions. Some institutions use a simple percentage of the trailing market value (e.g., 5% of the prior year's ending market value), which is easy to understand but can lead to volatile spending distributions that disrupt budget planning. Most sophisticated endowments use a geometric smoothing rule, such as a rolling average of market value over 12, 16, or 20 quarters, which dampens the impact of short-term market volatility on the operating budget, providing a stable and predictable stream of income. For example, if the market crashes by 30% in a single year, a simple percentage rule would slash the budget's endowment support by 30% the following year, forcing immediate, painful program cuts. A 20-quarter moving average, however, would only reduce the distribution by a few percentage points, allowing the institution to absorb the shock and maintain its core operations while the market recovers.

Some institutions combine a base payout with special reserve decisions or apply caps and floors to prevent wild swings in the operating budget, ensuring that spending does not grow unsustainably during bull markets or collapse during bear markets. Whatever formula is chosen, it should be stable, mathematically explainable, and reviewed periodically to ensure it remains appropriate for the institution's circumstances. Spending discipline matters because endowment promises are intertemporal, meaning the beneficiaries this year and the beneficiaries ten years from now both matter equally in the eyes of fiduciary duty. A policy that overpays in strong years and underperforms in weak years is not a true endowment policy; it is merely a market reaction mechanism that transfers market volatility directly into the institution's operating budget, undermining the very stability that the endowment is designed to provide.

9. Inflation and Intergenerational Equity

Inflation is one of the quietest, most persistent, and most destructive threats to endowment value, acting as a silent tax that continuously erodes the purchasing power of the capital base and the real value of the spending distributions. A nominal dollar spent today is not equivalent to a nominal dollar preserved for the future because inflation reduces the amount of goods and services that dollar can purchase, meaning that if an endowment merely maintains its nominal value over time, it is actually losing significant real value and failing in its duty to future generations. This is why long-term management cannot be measured only by nominal return; the fund must earn enough, after spending distributions and management fees, to support the current mission while preserving real, inflation-adjusted value, a concept known as intergenerational equity. UPMIFA’s prudence standard takes inflation into account for exactly this reason, requiring fiduciaries to consider the possible effect of inflation or deflation when making investment and spending decisions.

For institutions like universities and hospitals, the relevant inflation metric is often not the standard Consumer Price Index (CPI), but specialized indices like the Higher Education Price Index (HEPI) or the Higher Education Cost Project, which tracks the specific cost drivers of faculty salaries, technology, healthcare, and financial aid, which historically outpace general inflation by a significant margin. If an endowment targets a 2% real return based on CPI, but the institution's actual cost inflation is 4% (as measured by HEPI), the fund is effectively losing 2% of its real purchasing power every year, compounding into a massive shortfall over decades. Intergenerational equity is the moral and financial principle that current beneficiaries should not consume resources meant for future beneficiaries, ensuring that each generation inherits an institution that is at least as strong and well-resourced as the one they received.

For colleges, foundations, museums, hospitals, and religious organizations, this principle is central to their very existence and their claim to perpetual life. Endowments are built on the premise that each generation should be supported without permanently draining the capital base, creating a continuous chain of philanthropic impact that spans centuries. That is why spending rates, return expectations, and policy discipline must be assessed together, as a fund that looks generous in a single year can still be profoundly unfair if it leaves future stakeholders with a weaker institution and less real capital. Protecting purchasing power is not an act of greed or an attempt to hoard resources; it is an act of intergenerational justice, ensuring that the institution can continue to fulfill its mission for centuries to come, honoring the perpetual nature of the original donor gifts. This requires a relentless focus on net-of-fee, real returns, and a willingness to make difficult spending cuts during periods of high inflation to protect the long-term corpus.

10. Asset Allocation and Return Sources

Asset allocation is universally recognized as the single most important driver of endowment performance and risk, determining the vast majority of a portfolio's long-term return profile and its vulnerability to market shocks. Endowments commonly hold a highly diversified mix of public equities, fixed income, cash, private equity, venture capital, private credit, hedge or absolute-return strategies, and real assets (such as timber, real estate, natural resources, and infrastructure), depending on mission, time horizon, liquidity needs, and risk tolerance. This approach, heavily popularized by David Swensen at Yale University and often referred to as the "Endowment Model," relies heavily on equity orientation to capture the long-term growth premium and illiquid assets to capture the illiquidity premium, recognizing that endowments can afford to lock up capital for extended periods in exchange for higher expected returns. The point of allocation is not to chase the hottest asset class of the moment or to follow market fads, but to assemble a portfolio whose expected return, volatility, and liquidity profile match the fund’s spending obligation and tolerance for risk, creating a robust engine for long-term wealth generation.

Allocation is a governance choice as much as an investment choice, reflecting the board's judgment about the appropriate balance between risk and return, and its willingness to accept short-term volatility in pursuit of long-term mission support. A fund that spends heavily each year to support current operations cannot afford to lock too much capital into illiquid positions without a robust liquidity plan and sufficient cash reserves, as it may be forced to sell assets at a loss during a market downturn to meet its spending obligations. Conversely, a fund with a long horizon, stable donor support, and a strong operating budget may be able to take more illiquidity in pursuit of a higher return premium, leveraging its structural advantage to access asset classes that are unavailable to retail investors. The correct mix depends entirely on the institution’s purpose, obligations, and risk capacity, and must be tailored to the specific needs of the organization rather than copied from a peer institution.

Endowment management succeeds when allocation is chosen deliberately, based on rigorous analysis and a deep understanding of the institution's liabilities, monitored consistently to ensure it remains within policy limits, and revised only after careful review rather than after emotional reaction to market headlines or short-term performance pressures. The investment office must continuously evaluate the risk premia associated with each asset class, ensuring that the portfolio is compensated adequately for the risks it takes. For example, if the illiquidity premium in private equity compresses due to an influx of capital, the endowment may need to adjust its allocation, shifting toward public markets or alternative strategies that offer better risk-adjusted returns. This dynamic, research-driven approach to asset allocation is what separates sophisticated endowment managers from passive index huggers.

11. Diversification

Diversification reduces dependence on any single asset class, manager, strategy, or market regime, and in an endowment, that principle is not optional; it is a fiduciary mandate enshrined in modern portfolio theory and required by UPMIFA's prudence standard. The portfolio must survive many different environments: rising rates, falling rates, inflationary shocks, recession, equity booms, equity collapses, liquidity freezes, and valuation resets, and diversification is the primary tool for ensuring resilience in the face of these unpredictable macroeconomic forces. Diversification does not remove risk entirely, as all investments carry some degree of risk, but it can significantly reduce the chance that one adverse event destroys the spending stream or the capital base, ensuring that the institution can continue to fulfill its mission even in the most challenging economic conditions. Good diversification is not just about having many holdings or a long list of mutual funds, which can create a false sense of security; it is about owning exposures that behave differently when the macroeconomic world changes, providing a natural hedge against various types of market stress.

A diversified endowment thinks in terms of underlying return sources and risk factors rather than just security names, recognizing that different asset classes are driven by different economic variables and respond differently to changes in interest rates, inflation, and economic growth. That may mean combining growth assets with income assets, liquid assets with less liquid assets, and market beta with manager skill or alternative sources of return, creating a portfolio that is robust across a wide range of scenarios. The discipline is to prevent hidden concentration, which is a common pitfall where institutions believe they are diversified because they hold many different funds, but those funds are all heavily exposed to the same underlying risk factors or asset classes. Many endowments get into trouble not because they lack assets, but because they confuse variety with true diversification, holding dozens of long-only equity funds that all crash simultaneously during a market correction, or investing in multiple private equity funds that are all concentrated in the same vintage years or sectors.

True diversification requires rigorous correlation analysis, stress testing, and an understanding of how different asset classes perform under stress, ensuring that the portfolio is genuinely resilient and capable of weathering the inevitable storms of the financial markets. The investment committee must regularly review the portfolio's factor exposures, ensuring that it is not inadvertently taking on massive leverage or concentrated bets on a single economic outcome. For example, during the 2008 financial crisis, many institutions discovered that their supposedly diversified portfolios were all highly correlated to the same credit risk factors, leading to simultaneous collapses across public equities, private equity, and hedge funds. By focusing on true risk factor diversification, endowments can build portfolios that are antifragile, capable of not just surviving but thriving in a variety of economic environments.

12. Risk Budgeting

Risk budgeting asks how much risk the fund can afford to take in pursuit of return, and where that risk should be allocated across the portfolio, representing a sophisticated approach to risk management that goes beyond simple asset allocation. That question is especially important for endowments because the institution often depends heavily on the fund for operating support, meaning that excessive risk can lead to volatile spending distributions that disrupt program planning and undermine institutional stability. UPMIFA’s prudence framework implicitly requires this analysis because it asks fiduciaries to consider the institution’s purposes, resources, and economic conditions before choosing a course of action, ensuring that the level of risk taken is appropriate for the institution's specific circumstances and risk capacity. A practical risk budget includes market risk (the risk of losses due to broad market movements), liquidity risk (the risk of being unable to meet cash needs without selling assets at a loss), concentration risk (the risk of excessive exposure to a single asset, sector, or manager), manager risk (the risk that an external manager will underperform or fail), operational risk (the risk of losses due to internal process failures, human error, or fraud), and reputational risk (the risk of damage to the institution's reputation due to poor investment decisions or ethical lapses).

The board does not need to eliminate all risk; indeed, eliminating all risk guarantees that the endowment will lose purchasing power to inflation over time, failing in its duty to future generations. It needs to identify which risks are acceptable, which are prohibited, and which are tolerable only within strict limits, creating a clear framework for decision-making that aligns with the institution's risk appetite and mission objectives. A sound endowment program makes those tradeoffs explicit in policy and reporting, ensuring that the board and management have a clear understanding of the risks being taken and the expected returns for bearing those risks. That clarity matters because many institutional failures begin as unspoken risk assumptions, where the board assumes a certain level of safety that does not actually exist in the portfolio. If no one has defined how much drawdown, illiquidity, or tracking error is acceptable, then the organization is not managing risk; it is merely surviving it and hoping for the best, leaving it vulnerable to unexpected shocks that could have been mitigated with proper risk budgeting and planning.

Risk budgeting also involves setting explicit limits on leverage, derivative usage, and single-manager concentration, ensuring that no single point of failure can threaten the entire portfolio. The investment office must regularly report on the portfolio's risk metrics, such as Value at Risk (VaR), tracking error relative to the benchmark, and liquidity coverage ratios, providing the board with a transparent view of the risks being taken. By treating risk as a finite resource to be allocated deliberately, the endowment can optimize its risk-adjusted returns, ensuring that every unit of risk taken is compensated by an appropriate expected return, thereby maximizing the long-term value generated for the institution's mission.

13. Liquidity Management

Liquidity is the ability to meet cash needs without forcing disadvantageous sales of assets, and it is a critical component of endowment management that bridges the gap between investment design and mission delivery. Endowments often have infinite horizons, but they still must make near-term distributions to support the operating budget, pay management fees to external managers, support capital projects, respond to extraordinary institutional needs, and meet capital calls for private equity and venture capital commitments. That makes liquidity planning a core stewardship function, as a portfolio may appear incredibly strong on paper and still fail catastrophically if too much of it is locked in illiquid assets at the exact moment cash is required, forcing the institution to sell assets at fire-sale prices or borrow money at high interest rates. In that sense, liquidity is not a secondary issue or a mere operational detail; it is the lifeblood of the endowment, ensuring that the institution can meet its obligations without compromising the long-term integrity of the portfolio.

Liquidity management should be built into policy from the start, with the board knowing exactly what share of assets can be monetized within days, what share is tied up for years in private partnerships, and what resources are available if spending needs rise during a severe market downturn when liquidity is often most scarce and most expensive. The institution must also understand how capital calls for private equity, redemption gates for hedge funds, and valuation lags for real estate affect overall flexibility, ensuring that it does not overcommit to illiquid investments that could strain its cash flow in a stress scenario. A fund that cannot fund the annual operating budget without distressed sales of private assets is not positioned safely, regardless of how high its expected long-term return may be. The best endowment programs maintain sufficient liquid reserves and cash flow matching strategies, ensuring that they have enough cash and highly liquid public market assets to meet their near-term obligations, while still leaving room for higher-return strategies that justify less liquidity and capture the illiquidity premium.

This delicate balance requires careful forecasting of cash needs, regular stress testing of the liquidity profile, and a clear understanding of the tradeoffs between liquidity and return, ensuring that the institution can fulfill its mission in all market conditions. The investment office must maintain a detailed liquidity budget, mapping out all expected cash inflows (dividends, interest, maturing bonds, private market distributions) against all expected cash outflows (spending distributions, management fees, capital calls, operating transfers), ensuring that there is always a sufficient buffer to cover unexpected shortfalls. By proactively managing liquidity, the endowment avoids the catastrophic scenario of being forced to sell illiquid assets at a deep discount during a market panic, thereby protecting the long-term value of the corpus and ensuring the stability of the institution's operating budget.

14. Performance Measurement

Endowment performance should be measured against the objectives laid out in the IPS, not only against raw return numbers or arbitrary market indices, as the ultimate goal of the endowment is to support the institution's mission, not merely to achieve the highest possible return. A high return that comes with excessive volatility, hidden illiquidity, or policy drift may not be a success if it disrupts the operating budget or violates the institution's risk tolerance, while a lower return that is consistent with a conservative mission, stable spending, and limited risk may be entirely appropriate and highly successful. This is why board reporting should explicitly link performance to the IPS, the spending policy, the risk budget, and the institution’s mission, providing context and meaning to the raw numbers and helping the board understand whether the portfolio is truly serving its intended purpose. Numbers without context can severely mislead governance bodies, leading to poor decisions based on short-term performance chasing or an misunderstanding of the risks being taken.

Good performance review separates market movement (beta) from decision quality (alpha), asking whether the portfolio behaved as intended, whether external managers delivered value after fees, whether the strategic allocation still fits the policy, and whether current spending remains sustainable over the long term. Long-term results matter infinitely more than isolated annual outcomes, as the endowment is a long-term vehicle that must compound wealth over decades to maintain its purchasing power and support the mission. The common mistake is to treat a single year of strong performance as proof of genius or a single difficult year as proof of failure, leading to reactive changes in strategy that undermine long-term performance. Endowment stewardship should be slower, more deliberate, and more disciplined than that, focusing on the consistency of the process and the alignment of the outcomes with the long-term objectives.

The right question is whether the fund is compounding mission capacity in a way that can be defended over decades, utilizing metrics like the Internal Rate of Return (IRR) for private markets, which accounts for the timing of cash flows, and Time-Weighted Return (TWR) for public markets, which removes the impact of external cash flows to isolate the manager's performance, ensuring a fair and accurate assessment of investment results. Furthermore, performance must always be evaluated on a net-of-fees basis, as high gross returns can be entirely offset by excessive management fees and carried interest, leaving the endowment with subpar net results. By focusing on net, risk-adjusted, long-term returns, the board can ensure that the investment program is truly adding value to the institution and fulfilling its fiduciary duties.

15. Benchmarks and Peer Comparison

Benchmarks are essential because they give the board a rigorous frame of reference for judging results, providing an objective standard against which to measure the performance of the portfolio and the effectiveness of the investment strategy. A benchmark may be a broad market index, a custom policy mix that reflects the target asset allocation, a specific peer group of similar institutions, or a custom reference portfolio designed to capture the specific risk and return characteristics of the endowment. The Council on Foundations and the NACUBO-TIAA Endowment Study are widely used because they give boards a broad, empirical view of how similar institutions set objectives, allocate assets, and govern endowment capital, providing valuable context and insights into industry best practices. Benchmarks help distinguish true manager skill from the rising tide of a bull market, ensuring that the institution is not simply rewarding managers for market beta while paying alpha fees, and they make performance discussions more honest, data-driven, and less anecdotal.

Peer comparison is useful, but it can become highly misleading if context is ignored, as two institutions may both have "endowments" of similar size, yet one may face heavy, immediate scholarship demand, one may have a massive liquidity cushion, and another may be operating under donor terms that sharply constrain spending, meaning their appropriate risk and return profiles may be vastly different. A peer benchmark should inform judgment, not replace it, serving as a starting point for discussion rather than a definitive target to be chased at all costs. The most valuable comparison is not "Are we above the median of our peer group?" but "Are our policies and outcomes consistent with our own mission, liabilities, and restrictions?" That question is vastly better aligned with stewardship than with institutional status chasing, ensuring that the endowment is managed in the best interests of the institution rather than to win a popularity contest or achieve a ranking in a survey.

Effective benchmarking requires a deep understanding of the institution's unique circumstances, a clear articulation of the investment objectives, and a willingness to use custom benchmarks that accurately reflect the portfolio's risk profile, ensuring that the performance measurement process is meaningful, relevant, and aligned with the long-term goals of the endowment. For example, an endowment with a high allocation to private equity should not be benchmarked solely against the S&P 500, as the public market index will not capture the risk and return characteristics of the private holdings. Instead, a custom blended benchmark, incorporating public market indices and private market benchmarks (such as the Cambridge Associates Private Equity Index), should be used to provide a fair and accurate assessment of performance. By using appropriate, context-aware benchmarks, the board can make informed decisions about manager selection, asset allocation, and risk management, ensuring that the endowment remains on track to meet its long-term objectives.

16. Rebalancing Discipline

Rebalancing is the systematic process of returning the portfolio to its target allocation after market movement has pushed it off course, and it is one of the most powerful tools for enforcing discipline and maintaining the intended risk profile of the endowment. Without rebalancing, winning asset classes become larger than intended and risk accumulates in ways the board may never have approved, while losing asset classes are starved of capital and the portfolio becomes increasingly concentrated in the assets that have performed poorly, potentially violating the principles of diversification and risk management. A disciplined rebalancing policy is one of the simplest and strongest demonstrations of endowment governance because it turns policy into mechanical action, removing emotion from the decision-making process and ensuring that the institution adheres to its strategic asset allocation regardless of market conditions. It says, in effect, that the organization will not allow market drift to rewrite its risk posture, and that it will systematically buy low and sell high, capturing the returns from mean reversion and maintaining the desired risk-return profile.

Rebalancing should be systematic, not emotional, with the institution specifying thresholds (e.g., rebalance when an asset class deviates by more than 5% from its target), timing rules (e.g., rebalance quarterly or annually), responsible parties, and exception procedures. That way, rebalancing happens because policy says it should happen, not because someone feels uneasy about market headlines or is tempted to chase momentum. This matters especially after major rallies or severe drawdowns, when the temptation to override discipline and chase performance is strongest, and when the consequences of failing to rebalance can be most severe. In an endowment, the purpose of rebalancing is not to predict the next market turn or to time the market, which is a futile and often destructive endeavor. The purpose is to preserve the portfolio structure that the board determined was appropriate for the mission, ensuring that the portfolio remains aligned with the institution's long-term objectives and risk tolerance.

Furthermore, sophisticated endowments use cash flows—such as new donor contributions and annual spending distributions—to rebalance the portfolio efficiently, directing new money into underweight asset classes and using distributions from overweight asset classes to fund spending, thereby minimizing transaction costs, reducing market impact, and managing tax implications related to Unrelated Business Taxable Income (UBTI), ensuring that the rebalancing process is as efficient and cost-effective as possible. By using cash flows to rebalance, the endowment can maintain its target allocation without incurring the high trading costs and market disruption associated with selling existing holdings. This disciplined, cash-flow-driven approach to rebalancing is a hallmark of sophisticated endowment management, ensuring that the portfolio remains perfectly aligned with the IPS while minimizing the friction and costs associated with portfolio maintenance.

17. Underwater Endowments

An endowment is considered "underwater" when the fair value of the fund falls below the original amount that is required to be retained under donor stipulation or governing policy, a situation that is particularly important and politically sensitive after severe market declines, when the temptation is to maintain normal spending despite capital impairment. Underwater status creates a complex dilemma for institutional leaders, as they must balance the need to support current operations with the legal and moral obligation to preserve the corpus for future generations, often facing pressure from program managers to maintain spending levels even when the fund's value has significantly declined. UPMIFA’s prudence standard is highly relevant here because it does not treat underwater status as a simple excuse for either panic or complacency; instead, it requires careful, documented judgment about preservation, donor intent, and the organization’s ability to continue its purposes, considering the seven factors of prudence to determine the appropriate course of action.

Underwater funds require disciplined, transparent review, and the board may need to adjust the payout rate to preserve capital, use unrestricted reserves to support operations, reassess asset allocation to improve recovery prospects, or explain the situation more clearly to stakeholders and donors to manage expectations and maintain trust. The key is to avoid silent erosion, as spending normally while the fund is severely impaired can prolong the problem, mathematically reduce the chance of recovery, and violate the principle of intergenerational equity by consuming capital that belongs to future beneficiaries. Underwater management should be transparent, heavily documented, and conservative, with a clear rationale for any decisions made and a rigorous monitoring process to track the fund's recovery and the impact of spending decisions. A short-term desire to avoid visible pain or budget cuts can create a deeper long-term injury if the organization fails to respond to the condition the market has created, potentially leading to a downward spiral of declining capital and reduced spending capacity.

FASB ASU 2016-14 requires specific disclosures regarding the aggregate fair value of underwater endowment funds and the original gift amounts, ensuring that the public and donors are aware of these impairments and the institution's financial position, promoting transparency and accountability. Effective management of underwater endowments requires courage, discipline, and a long-term perspective, ensuring that the institution navigates the crisis without compromising its fiduciary duties or its ability to fulfill its mission in the future. The board must be willing to make difficult decisions, such as cutting spending or raising emergency funds, to protect the long-term integrity of the corpus, demonstrating to donors and stakeholders that the institution is a responsible steward of the resources entrusted to it, even in the face of severe market adversity.

18. Board-Designated Versus Donor-Restricted Funds

Board-designated funds and donor-restricted funds can look identical in high-level summary reports and investment pool summaries, but they are fundamentally different legal and accounting entities with vastly different implications for institutional flexibility and fiduciary duty. Board-designated funds (quasi-endowments) are set aside internally by the governing body and generally remain under board control, meaning the board can reverse the designation and use the funds for other purposes if necessary, providing a crucial source of financial flexibility during times of institutional stress or strategic opportunity. Donor-restricted funds, on the other hand, are governed by the strict terms of the contribution and, in many cases, by state trust laws and accounting rules, meaning the institution has no legal right to use the funds for any purpose other than that specified by the donor, and any deviation requires legal intervention or donor consent. IRS and FASB guidance distinguish these categories because users of the financial statements need to know what is truly available for discretionary spending and what is legally locked away, ensuring that the financial statements accurately reflect the institution's financial position and the nature of its resources.

The management implication is simple but vitally important: flexibility is not just an accounting label, and restriction is not just a moral preference; they represent fundamentally different legal realities that must be respected and managed accordingly. A board-designated reserve can often be repurposed to save the institution during an existential crisis, but that does not make it safe to spend casually on routine operating deficits or to treat it as a slush fund for pet projects; it should still be managed with a long-term perspective and subject to rigorous governance. A donor-restricted endowment should be treated with a vastly higher standard of care because its use is bounded by the donor’s conditions and the organization’s legal obligations, and any misuse can result in legal action by the state attorney general, loss of donor trust, and severe reputational damage. The better the classification system, the lower the risk of accidental misuse, legal liability, or misleading public communication, ensuring that the institution maintains the confidence of its donors, regulators, and the public.

Institutions must maintain meticulous sub-ledgers that track the original corpus, accumulated income, and accumulated gains for every individual restricted fund, ensuring that they can accurately report on the status of each fund, comply with donor restrictions, and demonstrate their fiduciary responsibility in managing the resources entrusted to them. This granular level of tracking is essential for audit readiness and for ensuring that the institution can provide detailed impact reports to donors, showing exactly how their specific gifts have been invested and spent in accordance with their intentions. By maintaining a clear, auditable distinction between board-designated and donor-restricted funds, the institution protects itself from legal risk, enhances its transparency, and ensures that it can deploy its financial resources effectively and ethically.

19. Net Assets and Endowment Classification

FASB’s not-for-profit reporting framework, particularly the landmark ASU 2016-14, radically reshaped the presentation of net assets and the disclosures required around endowment funds, simplifying the classification system while enhancing transparency and requiring more detailed disclosures about the composition and governance of endowment capital. The basic point of this standard is that financial statements should help readers clearly see how donor restrictions affect the organization’s resources and what portion of endowment capital is subject to preservation, spending policy, or other limitations, replacing the old three-class system (unrestricted, temporarily restricted, permanently restricted) with a two-class system (net assets with donor restrictions and net assets without donor restrictions) that focuses on the presence or absence of donor-imposed restrictions. Good accounting here is not merely a compliance exercise; it is a vital transparency tool that helps the board, donors, rating agencies, and the public understand the institution’s real financial posture and the true availability of its resources for mission support.

This classification matters because the same endowment investment portfolio can contain multiple layers of legal and operational meaning, with some assets supporting permanently restricted principal, some representing accumulated gains available for spending under policy, and some belonging to board designation, all of which must be accurately reflected in the financial statements to provide a complete picture of the institution's financial health. The notes to the financial statements, especially those tied to endowment disclosures and liquidity availability, are therefore just as important as the balance sheet headline, providing detailed information about the institution's spending policy, the composition of the endowment, the amount of underwater funds, and the governance processes in place to manage the endowment. Accurate classification protects credibility, supports auditability, and prevents the institution from overstating how much of its wealth is actually available for current use, ensuring that stakeholders have a realistic understanding of the institution's financial capacity and constraints.

It forces management to reconcile the investment pool with the legal restrictions attached to the capital, ensuring that the financial statements accurately reflect the economic reality of the endowment and the institution's obligations to its donors and beneficiaries. By providing a clear and transparent view of the endowment's composition and governance, ASU 2016-14 enhances the usefulness of the financial statements for decision-making and strengthens the accountability of the institution to its stakeholders. The institution must work closely with its auditors to ensure that the classification of net assets is accurate, that the disclosures are comprehensive, and that the financial statements tell a clear, consistent story about the endowment's role in supporting the institution's mission.

20. Statement of Financial Position

The statement of financial position is the nonprofit equivalent of a corporate balance sheet, providing a snapshot of the institution's assets, liabilities, and net assets at a specific point in time, and for endowment management, it reveals the sheer size of the investment pool, the relation of assets to liabilities (such as annuities or trusts payable), and the strict classification of net assets. It is the first place many board members look to understand whether the organization is financially stable, but it should never be read in isolation, as the raw numbers only tell part of the story and must be interpreted in the context of the institution's mission, spending policy, and the detailed footnotes that explain the nature of the assets and restrictions. The statement becomes meaningful only when paired with the extensive footnotes and with the policies governing endowment use, which provide the necessary context to understand the liquidity, restrictions, and risk profile of the endowment assets.

Boards should read this statement with three critical questions in mind. First, how much of the portfolio is actually available for operations without violating donor intent, and what is the timing and nature of those restrictions? Second, what restrictions, lock-ups, or obligations limit that availability, and how do they impact the institution's ability to meet its near-term cash needs and strategic objectives? Third, how does the size and composition of the endowment compare with the institution’s long-term spending needs, debt structure, and deferred maintenance liabilities, and is the endowment adequately sized to support the institution's mission over the long term? Those questions turn a static statement into a dynamic governance tool, helping the board assess the financial sustainability of the institution and the adequacy of the endowment to support its strategic goals. A strong board does not simply admire asset size; it asks what the balance sheet can really support, and for how long, under current policy and stress scenarios, ensuring that the institution is not overextending itself based on an unrealistic assessment of its available resources.

Furthermore, the valuation of alternative investments, often reported at Net Asset Value (NAV) as a practical expedient under FASB guidance, requires intense scrutiny to ensure the institution is not carrying stale or inflated values, as the liquidity and realizable value of these assets may be significantly different from their reported NAV, especially during periods of market stress. By critically analyzing the statement of financial position and understanding the nuances of the endowment assets, the board can make informed decisions about the institution's financial strategy and ensure its long-term viability. The board must ensure that the valuation policies for alternative investments are robust, that the auditors are rigorously testing the NAV reports, and that the institution is not relying on illiquid, hard-to-value assets to meet near-term cash obligations.

21. Statement of Activities

The statement of activities shows how revenues, expenses, gains, losses, and releases from restriction change the organization’s net assets over time, providing a dynamic view of the institution's financial performance and the flow of resources during the reporting period. In an endowment context, this statement helps the board see whether investment income, realized gains, unrealized appreciation, and distribution policy are aligning with the mission and the operating model, revealing how the endowment is contributing to the institution's overall financial health and mission support. It is not enough to know the fund is large; the board must see whether the organization is drawing on the fund in a way that is consistent with its long-term sustainability, and whether the investment returns are sufficient to cover the spending distributions, management fees, and inflation, thereby preserving the real value of the corpus.

One of the most useful habits in endowment oversight is to separate operating strength from investment strength, as a strong endowment can easily mask a weak, structurally deficient operating model if leaders are not careful, leading to complacency and a failure to address fundamental operational issues. Likewise, a strong operating year driven by tuition, grants, or program revenue does not erase a weak endowment year, and the institution must ensure that it is not relying solely on current operations to fund its mission while neglecting the long-term growth of the endowment. The statement of activities helps distinguish those stories, showing whether mission support is being delivered through disciplined spending from the endowment, whether restriction releases are being managed honestly and accurately in accordance with donor intent and accounting standards, and whether the endowment is genuinely enhancing mission capacity or merely compensating for structural budget strain and operational inefficiencies.

By analyzing the statement of activities, the board can assess the sustainability of the institution's financial model, the effectiveness of its fundraising and investment strategies, and the alignment of its resource allocation with its strategic priorities, ensuring that the institution is making the most of its resources to fulfill its mission. The board should pay close attention to the line items for unrealized appreciation and depreciation, understanding that these are non-cash items that do not affect the institution's liquidity but do impact the reported net assets. By separating the operating statement from the investment statement, the board can gain a clear understanding of the institution's true operational performance and its reliance on the endowment for ongoing support.

22. Statement of Cash Flows

The statement of cash flows is especially important because paper gains and unrealized appreciation are not the same thing as spendable cash, and an institution can be profitable on paper while facing a severe cash crunch if it does not manage its liquidity effectively. Endowment portfolios may show incredibly strong valuation gains on private equity or real estate while still needing careful cash management for spending distributions, management fees, and capital calls, meaning that the institution must ensure it has sufficient liquid assets to meet its near-term obligations without being forced to sell illiquid assets at a disadvantage. The cash-flow statement helps the board see whether the organization is generating operating cash from its core programs and services, depending on investment liquidations to fund operations, or using financing sources to bridge gaps, providing a clear picture of the institution's cash generation and consumption patterns.

That information is essential for a true understanding of liquidity and sustainability, as it reveals whether the institution is living within its means and generating sufficient cash to support its operations, or whether it is relying on asset sales or borrowing to fund its activities, which is not a sustainable long-term strategy. Boards should use the cash-flow statement to test whether the organization is living within its means, and if operations consistently consume cash faster than the institution generates it from programmatic revenue, then the endowment may be covering a structural problem rather than simply supporting mission, which can lead to the erosion of the endowment's real value over time. The statement helps reveal whether endowment distributions are supplementing healthy operations or quietly subsidizing recurring, unsustainable deficits, a distinction that matters profoundly because an endowment is supposed to enhance mission capacity and provide a buffer against shocks, not conceal chronic operating weakness or fund structural imbalances.

By analyzing the cash flow from operating, investing, and financing activities, the board can assess the institution's cash management practices, its reliance on the endowment for operating support, and its ability to generate sufficient cash to meet its obligations, ensuring that the institution maintains a healthy cash position and a sustainable financial model. The board must ensure that the institution has a robust cash management policy, that it is forecasting its cash needs accurately, and that it is maintaining sufficient liquid reserves to cover unexpected shortfalls without resorting to the distressed sale of illiquid endowment assets.

23. Statement of Functional Expenses

For many nonprofits, the statement of functional expenses is one of the clearest windows into how resources are actually deployed, separating costs among program, management and general, and fundraising categories, and providing valuable insights into the institution's operational efficiency and resource allocation priorities. In endowment management, this statement helps the board evaluate whether the institution is using endowment income to support core mission delivery or to absorb structural inefficiency and administrative bloat, ensuring that the endowment is being used to advance the institution's primary purpose rather than to subsidize overhead or fundraising costs. It also helps donors understand the relation between spending and impact, as they want to see that their contributions are being used effectively to support programs and services, rather than being consumed by administrative expenses.

Functional expense reporting should not be used to create a simplistic, punitive "good expense, bad expense" mindset, as administration, fundraising, and governance are necessary, vital parts of a healthy, sustainable institution that enable it to deliver its programs effectively and raise the resources needed to fulfill its mission. The real question is whether the overall spending pattern is aligned with mission and whether endowment support is being used to strengthen durable programs rather than merely postponing hard decisions about operational efficiency or program prioritization. A disciplined board reads this statement as a diagnostic tool, not as a scoreboard for vanity, using it to identify areas where costs can be reduced, where investments in infrastructure are needed, and where the institution can improve its operational efficiency to maximize the impact of its resources.

Furthermore, the allocation of endowment management fees and investment expenses must be handled carefully in accordance with accounting standards; while often netted against investment return, some administrative costs related to endowment management may need to be classified as management and general expenses, impacting the institution's overhead ratios and potentially affecting its ability to recover indirect costs from grants and contracts. By analyzing the statement of functional expenses, the board can ensure that the institution is using its resources effectively, maintaining a reasonable balance between program and administrative expenses, and maximizing the impact of its endowment and other resources in support of its mission. The board must ensure that the expense allocation methodology is transparent, consistent, and in compliance with GAAP, ensuring that the financial statements accurately reflect the true cost of the institution's operations.

24. Form 990 and Schedule D

For many nonprofits, Form 990 is the main public window into endowment stewardship, and Schedule D (Part V) is the key supplemental schedule for endowment reporting, providing detailed information about the institution's endowment funds, including the beginning and ending balances, contributions, investment earnings or losses, amounts appropriated for expenditure, administrative expenses, and the composition of the endowment by net asset class. The IRS instructions are explicit that organizations must report this information accurately and completely, as it is far more than compliance paperwork; it is the public record through which outsiders, donors, watchdog groups, and state regulators evaluate whether the organization is managing its capital responsibly and in accordance with its tax-exempt status. That public layer matters because inconsistency creates trust problems incredibly fast, and if the internal policy says one thing, the audited financial statements say another, and the tax filing says a third, the organization has created massive confusion and suspicion even before any regulator intervenes, potentially damaging its reputation and its ability to attract future donations.

Endowment management should therefore integrate accounting, tax, and disclosure from the very start, ensuring that the information reported on the Form 990 is consistent with the financial statements and the institution's internal policies, and that it accurately reflects the nature and governance of the endowment funds. The best systems are those in which the board can explain the fund in one consistent, unbroken story across policy, audit, and public filing, demonstrating transparency, accountability, and a commitment to good governance. Furthermore, the 990 requires disclosure of the organization's spending policy and whether it has complied with it, making the IPS a matter of public record and subjecting the institution's spending decisions to public scrutiny, which reinforces the need for a prudent, well-documented spending policy that can be defended to donors, regulators, and the public.

By carefully preparing and reviewing the Form 990 and Schedule D, the institution ensures that it meets its regulatory obligations, maintains the trust of its stakeholders, and demonstrates its commitment to responsible stewardship of its endowment resources. The board and management must work closely with their tax advisors to ensure that the 990 is accurate, complete, and filed on time, avoiding any penalties or negative publicity that could arise from errors or omissions. The 990 is not just a tax form; it is a public relations document that tells the story of the institution's financial health and its commitment to its mission, and it must be treated with the same care and attention as the audited financial statements.

25. Internal Controls and Segregation of Duties

Endowment assets are often large enough to require exceptionally strong internal controls to safeguard the resources, prevent fraud and error, and ensure the accuracy and reliability of financial reporting. The control environment should rigorously separate authorization, custody, recordkeeping, and reconciliation so that no single person can move money, record the transaction, and approve the outcome without independent review, creating a system of checks and balances that reduces the risk of misuse and ensures that all transactions are properly authorized and documented. Even where the sources focus primarily on legal and reporting frameworks, those frameworks assume a control structure capable of supporting prudence and reliability, as good governance and sound investment policies are meaningless without effective internal controls to implement and monitor them.

Stewardship without control is only aspiration, and institutions must implement practical controls such as dual approval for all wire transfers, monthly reconciliations of custodian statements, strict access restrictions to trading platforms, documentation of all exceptions, and periodic, independent review of valuation or distribution calculations to ensure the integrity of the endowment management process. For alternative investments, which are often complex and illiquid, controls must include verifying capital calls, tracking distributions, monitoring the financial health of the general partners, and ensuring that the valuations reported by the fund managers are reasonable and supported by appropriate documentation. Strong controls are not a sign of distrust in staff; they are a sign that the organization understands the severe consequences of error and fraud, and that it is committed to protecting the resources entrusted to it by its donors and beneficiaries.

They also make the audit process vastly smoother and make board oversight more meaningful because reports can be trusted implicitly, allowing the board to focus on strategic issues rather than getting bogged down in operational details. The point of control is to create an environment in which the right behavior is the easy behavior and the wrong behavior is virtually impossible to hide, ensuring that the institution maintains the highest standards of integrity and accountability in its management of the endowment. The board must regularly review the internal control environment, ensuring that it is robust, effective, and adapted to the changing risks facing the institution, and that there is a clear tone at the top that emphasizes the importance of integrity and compliance.

26. Audit Readiness

Audit readiness means the organization can produce clean records, supporting schedules, policy documents, reconciliations, and disclosure notes without scrambling at year-end, ensuring that the annual audit is a smooth, efficient process that confirms the accuracy of the financial statements rather than a frantic rescue mission to fix errors and missing documentation. Because endowment accounting requires careful classification of net assets, fair value measurement (especially for Level 3 assets such as private equity and real estate), and extensive disclosure about the governance and composition of the endowment, records should be maintained in a way that makes the annual audit a confirmation of good order rather than a stressful and time-consuming ordeal. This matters for both internal confidence and external credibility, as a board that can explain the fund only after a massive, stressful reconciliation exercise is not yet exercising strong governance, and the resulting delays and potential restatements can damage the institution's reputation and its relationship with donors and regulators.

A good audit-ready endowment function can answer basic questions quickly: what is donor-restricted, what is board-designated, what spending rule is in force, how gains are allocated, what underwater amounts exist, and how endowment activity is reflected in the statements and filing, providing the auditors with the information they need to complete their work efficiently and effectively. When those answers are documented in advance and supported by robust internal controls, the audit becomes a structured review instead of a reconstruction exercise, reducing audit fees, minimizing the risk of audit adjustments, and providing assurance to the board and stakeholders that the financial statements are reliable. The organization also benefits operationally because staff spend less time untangling past decisions and more time maintaining current discipline and focusing on strategic allocation, improving the overall efficiency and effectiveness of the endowment management function.

By maintaining a state of continuous audit readiness, the institution demonstrates its commitment to transparency, accountability, and good governance, ensuring that it can meet its reporting obligations and maintain the trust of its stakeholders. The investment office and the controller's office must work closely together throughout the year, ensuring that all transactions are recorded accurately, that all valuations are supported by appropriate documentation, and that all disclosures are prepared in accordance with GAAP. By treating audit readiness as a year-round priority rather than a year-end scramble, the institution can ensure that its financial statements are a true and fair reflection of its financial position and its commitment to its mission.

27. Transparency and Donor Communication

Donors contribute to endowments because they expect durable, perpetual mission support, and they do not expect opacity or evasion; they want to know that their gifts are being used effectively and in accordance with their intentions. That is why transparency matters both legally and relationally, as it is essential for building and maintaining the trust that is the foundation of all philanthropic relationships. NACUBO, the Council on Foundations, and the National Council of Nonprofits all frame endowment management as stewardship, and the disclosure systems in FASB and IRS reporting exist because users need to know how endowment capital is held, spent, and preserved, ensuring that the institution is accountable to its donors and the public. A trustworthy organization proactively explains what the endowment does, how the spending policy works, and why legal restrictions matter, providing clear, accessible information that helps donors understand the impact of their gifts and the long-term sustainability of the institution.

Good donor communication prevents common, damaging misunderstandings, such as the misconception that an endowment is a pile of idle cash sitting in a bank account that can be spent immediately, or that all endowment dollars are available for immediate use, when in fact much of the capital is restricted or must be preserved to maintain the fund's purchasing power. It also helps donors understand why a conservative spending rule may actually be a sign of profound respect for their intent, ensuring that their gifts will continue to support the mission for generations to come, rather than being exhausted in a few years. The best communication is plain, consistent, and tied directly to policy, avoiding jargon and technical language that can confuse or alienate donors, and instead focusing on the impact of their gifts and the institution's commitment to responsible stewardship.

It does not try to impress with jargon; it tries to clarify, building trust through honesty and transparency. That clarity deepens trust, encourages future giving, and reduces pressure on leaders to make poor policy decisions based on public perception or temporary emotional demands, ensuring that the endowment is managed in the best long-term interests of the institution and its beneficiaries. The institution must provide regular, detailed impact reports to donors, showing exactly how their gifts have been invested, how much income has been generated, and how that income has been spent to support the specific purposes outlined in the gift agreement. By maintaining open, honest, and transparent communication with donors, the institution can build a strong, lasting relationship that supports its mission for generations to come.

28. Scenario Planning and Stress Testing

Endowments should be rigorously tested against adverse scenarios such as severe market declines, inflation spikes, prolonged weak returns (secular stagnation), illiquidity crunches, and unexpected spending pressure, ensuring that the institution is prepared for a wide range of potential future outcomes and can withstand significant financial shocks without compromising its mission. UPMIFA’s prudence standard strongly supports this kind of forward-looking discipline because it requires consideration of conditions and circumstances rather than blind reliance on past historical averages, recognizing that the future is uncertain and that the institution must be prepared for the unexpected. Scenario planning is therefore not an optional enhancement or an academic exercise; it is a mandatory part of the logic of prudent management, providing a structured approach to assessing the resilience of the endowment and the institution's financial model under stress.
